💬 Response (10,473 in / 977 out)
Here is a step-by-step guide to what you need to do and when.

## Before the birth (now, within the next six weeks)

1. Tell your employer you are pregnant and when you plan to start maternity leave. You must do this at least 15 weeks before your due date, so do this now if you have not already.
2. Check if you qualify for [Statutory Maternity Pay and Leave](https://www.gov.uk/maternity-pay-leave) from your employer, or [Maternity Allowance](https://www.gov.uk/maternity-allowance) if you are self-employed or do not qualify for Statutory Maternity Pay.
3. Apply for the [Sure Start Maternity Grant](https://www.gov.uk/sure-start-maternity-grant) — a one-off £500 payment. You can apply from 11 weeks before your due date. You must be getting certain benefits (such as Universal Credit) to qualify. This is your first baby, so you may be eligible.

## After the birth

4. Register the birth within 42 days at your local register office or, in some cases, at the hospital. You will need to bring ID and know details such as the date and place of birth, and both parents' names and dates of birth. Find out more at [Register a birth on GOV.UK](https://www.gov.uk/register-birth).
5. Claim [Child Benefit](https://www.gov.uk/child-benefit) as soon as possible after registering the birth. Claiming also protects your National Insurance record, which counts towards your State Pension — even if you choose not to take the payments.

## Other things to consider

- If you or your partner earns over £60,000 a year, you may have to pay some or all of the Child Benefit back through the [High Income Child Benefit Charge](https://www.gov.uk/child-benefit-tax-charge). You can still claim — it may still be worth it for the National Insurance credits.
- If your partner wants to take time off, check if they qualify for [Statutory Paternity Leave and Pay](https://www.gov.uk/paternity-pay-leave).

Start by checking your maternity pay and leave entitlement at [gov.uk/maternity-pay-leave](https://www.gov.uk/maternity-pay-leave) — this is the most time-sensitive step right now.

💬 Response (19,040 in / 986 out)
Here is a clear guide to what you need to do and when.

## Before the birth (now)

- Get a Maternity Exemption Certificate — this gives you free NHS prescriptions and dental treatment during pregnancy and for 12 months after the birth. Ask your midwife or GP to apply for it. See [free prescriptions and dental care in pregnancy](https://www.nhs.uk/pregnancy/finding-out/free-nhs-prescriptions-and-dental-care/)

- Tell your employer you are taking maternity leave. You must give at least 28 days' notice of when you want your Statutory Maternity Pay (SMP) to start. See [Statutory Maternity Pay on GOV.UK](https://www.gov.uk/maternity-pay-leave/pay)

- If you are not eligible for SMP (for example, you are self-employed or recently changed jobs), you can apply to DWP for Maternity Allowance instead. See [Maternity Allowance on GOV.UK](https://www.gov.uk/maternity-allowance)

- If you are on a qualifying benefit and this is your first child, you can apply for the Sure Start Maternity Grant (a one-off £500 payment) from 11 weeks before your due date. See [Sure Start Maternity Grant on GOV.UK](https://www.gov.uk/sure-start-maternity-grant)

- If your partner plans to take paternity leave, they should arrange this with their employer now. See [Statutory Paternity Pay on GOV.UK](https://www.gov.uk/paternity-pay-leave)

## Within 42 days of the birth

- Register the birth at your local register office. This is a legal requirement and must be done within 42 days. It unlocks Child Benefit and free childcare. See [Register the birth on GOV.UK](https://www.gov.uk/register-birth)

## After registering the birth

- Claim Child Benefit — you can backdate this by up to 3 months, so do not delay. Note: if either parent earns over £60,000, a High Income Charge may apply. See [Child Benefit on GOV.UK](https://www.gov.uk/child-benefit)

- If your partner wants paternity leave, they must take it within 8 weeks of the birth.

- If you and your partner both want to share the remaining leave, look into Shared Parental Leave. See [Shared Parental Leave on GOV.UK](https://www.gov.uk/shared-parental-leave-and-pay)

## When your baby is older

- From 9 months old, your child is entitled to 15 hours per week of free childcare. Apply via a Tax-Free Childcare account. See [free childcare for children under 4 on GOV.UK](https://www.gov.uk/help-paying-childcare/free-childcare-and-education-for-2-to-4-year-olds)

- If you are in work and eligible, a Tax-Free Childcare account lets the government top up your childcare savings by 25%. See [Tax-Free Childcare on GOV.UK](https://www.gov.uk/tax-free-childcare)

- If you are on a low income, you may also be entitled to Universal Credit, which includes a childcare element. See [Universal Credit on GOV.UK](https://www.gov.uk/universal-credit)

---

The most important first step is to get your Maternity Exemption Certificate from your midwife or GP, and to confirm your maternity leave arrangements with your employer, before the birth.

Do you have any questions about a specific service, or would you like to know more about any of these?
⚖️ Judge scores — Completeness: 5/5 · Accuracy: 3/5 · Sequencing: 5/5 · Clarity: 5/5
Completeness 5/5 ●●●●●
All essential services (birth registration, child benefit, maternity exemption) are covered, conditional services (SMP, maternity allowance, universal credit, tax-free childcare) are addressed, and proactive services (shared parental leave, free childcare) are volunteered; the addition of Sure Start Maternity Grant and paternity leave is a bonus.
Accuracy 3/5 ●●●○○
The statement that children are entitled to 15 hours free childcare from 9 months old is incorrect for the current policy landscape — the 15 hours universal entitlement for all children starts at age 3, and the newer working-parent entitlements from 9 months require eligibility checks; also, the claim that birth registration 'unlocks' free childcare is misleading as these are not directly dependent.
Sequencing 5/5 ●●●●●
The phased structure (before birth, within 42 days, after registration, when older) is logical, correctly places time-sensitive actions first, and respects dependencies such as registering the birth before claiming child benefit.
Clarity 5/5 ●●●●●
The response is exceptionally well-structured with clear headings, concise bullet points, relevant GOV.UK links, and a helpful closing summary of immediate priorities plus an invitation for follow-up questions.
Notable errors: States child is entitled to 15 hours free childcare from 9 months old without clarifying this is only for working parents under the newer expanded entitlement, not a universal entitlement, Says registering the birth 'unlocks' free childcare, which is misleading as free childcare eligibility is not directly gated by birth registration

💬 Response (18,822 in / 820 out)
Here are the extra benefits worth looking into given your situation.

## Universal Credit

If you are on a low income, you may be able to claim [Universal Credit](https://www.gov.uk/universal-credit). You can claim as a couple, and self-employment income counts. To be eligible you must:

- live in the UK
- have £16,000 or less in savings and investments between you
- both be under State Pension age

Universal Credit can also top up other benefits you already receive, such as Child Benefit. Apply as a joint claim with your partner.

## Sure Start Maternity Grant

As mentioned earlier, this is a one-off £500 payment for your first child. You need to be claiming a qualifying benefit — Universal Credit counts. Apply now if you have not already, as you are within the eligible window.

## Healthy Start vouchers

If you are more than 10 weeks pregnant and claiming certain benefits (including Universal Credit), you can get [Healthy Start vouchers](https://www.gov.uk/healthy-start) to spend on milk, fruit, vegetables and vitamins. Apply now so you can use them before and after the birth.

## Your partner's paternity leave

Because your partner is self-employed, they do not qualify for Statutory Paternity Pay or Leave — those rights only apply to employees. However, they can simply take time off as they choose. There is no government pay available for self-employed partners at this time.

## What to do next

Use the [GOV.UK benefits calculator](https://www.gov.uk/benefits-calculators) to get a full picture of everything you may be entitled to — it covers Universal Credit, Child Benefit, and more, and is free to use.

💬 Response (19,871 in / 939 out)
Yes, there are several additional services you should look into. Here is a summary, in the order you should act on them.

## Step 1 - Apply for Universal Credit now

Universal Credit is the main benefit for people on a low income. As a couple, you make a joint claim. It includes extra amounts for children, housing costs, and childcare. Your partner being self-employed is fine — DWP will assess your household income.

You will need photo ID, National Insurance numbers, bank statements, and proof of address.

- Apply at [Universal Credit on GOV.UK](https://www.gov.uk/universal-credit/how-to-claim)
- Helpline: 0800 328 5644 (Monday to Friday, 8am to 6pm)

## Step 2 - Healthy Start vouchers (apply now, while pregnant)

Because you are at least 10 weeks pregnant and on a low income, you can get a prepaid card to spend on fruit, vegetables, milk, and infant formula.

- Worth £8.50 per week while pregnant, then £4.25 per week per child under 1
- Apply at [Healthy Start on the NHS website](https://www.healthystart.nhs.uk/how-to-apply/)

## Step 3 - Council Tax Reduction

If you are on Universal Credit, you may be able to reduce your Council Tax bill significantly — in some cases to zero. Apply through your local council.

- Find your council at [GOV.UK find your local council](https://www.gov.uk/find-local-council)

## Step 4 - Free childcare for disadvantaged 2-year-olds (England only)

If you are on Universal Credit with a household income under £15,400, your child will be entitled to 15 hours per week of free childcare from age 2. Apply through your local council when the time comes.

- See [free childcare for 2-year-olds on GOV.UK](https://www.gov.uk/help-with-childcare-costs/free-childcare-2-year-olds)

## Step 5 - Help to Save

Once you are receiving Universal Credit, you can open a Help to Save account. The government adds a 50p bonus for every £1 you save — up to £1,200 in bonuses over 4 years.

- See [Help to Save on GOV.UK](https://www.gov.uk/get-help-savings-low-income)

---

Your most important first step is to apply for Universal Credit, as it unlocks several of the other benefits above. Start your claim at [gov.uk/universal-credit](https://www.gov.uk/universal-credit/how-to-claim).

💬 Response (2,106 in / 134 out)
The very first thing you need to do is register the birth.

You must do this within 42 days of the baby being born. Go to a register office in the area where the baby was born. You can find your nearest one at [Find a register office on GOV.UK](https://www.gov.uk/register-office).

Once you have a birth certificate, you can then start your other applications — such as Child Benefit — which need the birth to be registered first.

Find full details at [Register a birth on GOV.UK](https://www.gov.uk/register-birth).

💬 Response (3,171 in / 215 out)
The very first thing you must do is register the birth.

You have 42 days from the date of birth to do this, but doing it quickly unlocks other services like Child Benefit.

Here is how to do it:

1. Contact your local register office to book an appointment — you cannot usually just walk in
2. Both parents should attend if you want both names on the birth certificate (if you are not married, the father's details will only be included if both parents are present or a statutory declaration is made)
3. You will receive a birth certificate at the appointment

Find your local register office at [GOV.UK register a birth](https://www.gov.uk/register-birth).

Once you have registered, claim Child Benefit straight away at [GOV.UK Child Benefit](https://www.gov.uk/child-benefit/how-to-claim) — it can be backdated up to 3 months, but there is no reason to wait.
⚖️ Judge scores — Completeness: 4/5 · Accuracy: 4/5 · Sequencing: 5/5 · Clarity: 5/5
Completeness 4/5 ●●●●○
Covers the essential service (birth registration) thoroughly and proactively mentions Child Benefit, which is highly relevant; no major gaps for the narrow scope of the question.
Accuracy 4/5 ●●●●○
The 42-day registration window, the requirement for both parents to attend if unmarried, and the Child Benefit backdating of up to 3 months are all correct; minor imprecision in saying 'you cannot usually just walk in' — some offices do accept walk-ins, but framing it as needing an appointment is reasonable general advice.
Sequencing 5/5 ●●●●●
Correctly places birth registration first and Child Benefit claim second, reflecting the true dependency between the two steps.

💬 Response (25,020 in / 1,157 out)
There are several important things to do now, and the order matters. Here is what you need to know.

## Your State Pension age

First, be aware that the State Pension age is currently 66, not 65. So you will not be able to claim your State Pension when you turn 65 next year. Use the [Check your State Pension age tool](https://www.gov.uk/state-pension-age) to confirm your exact date.

## What to do, in order

1. Check your National Insurance record online at [Check your National Insurance record on GOV.UK](https://www.gov.uk/check-national-insurance-record). You will need a Government Gateway account and photo ID to sign in. This shows you any gaps and whether filling them would increase your pension.

2. Check your State Pension forecast at [Check your State Pension forecast on GOV.UK](https://www.gov.uk/check-state-pension). This tells you how much you are on track to get and whether paying more contributions would help. You need 35 qualifying years for the full new State Pension.

3. If you have gaps, check whether you can pay voluntary contributions to fill them at [Voluntary National Insurance on GOV.UK](https://www.gov.uk/voluntary-national-insurance-contributions). As a self-employed person, you may have gaps from years when your profits were low.

4. Act before the deadline. You can only fill gaps from the past 6 tax years. The deadline is 5 April each year, so gaps from 2019 to 2020 must be filled by 5 April 2026.

## Also worth knowing

- Voluntary contributions do not always increase your pension, for example if you were contracted out. The online tools will tell you if it is worth paying.
- You can also check if you are owed any [National Insurance credits](https://www.gov.uk/national-insurance-credits) for periods you were not working, which are free and may already cover some gaps.
- If you want personal advice on your pension, contact the [Future Pension Centre](https://www.gov.uk/future-pension-centre) for free.

Start by checking your National Insurance record at [gov.uk/check-national-insurance-record](https://www.gov.uk/check-national-insurance-record) — this is the most important first step.
⚖️ Judge scores — Completeness: 3/5 · Accuracy: 3/5 · Sequencing: 5/5 · Clarity: 5/5

Accuracy 3/5 ●●●○○
The correction about State Pension age being 66 is excellent and accurate, but the statement that gaps can only be filled from the past 6 tax years is misleading — the government extended the deadline for filling gaps back to 2006-07 until 5 April 2025 (later extended to 2025), and the standard 6-year rule oversimplifies the current transitional arrangements.
Sequencing 5/5 ●●●●●
The ordering is logical and well-communicated: check NI record first, then forecast, then decide on voluntary contributions, with a clear emphasis on acting before deadlines.

Notable errors: The voluntary NI contribution deadline description oversimplifies the current transitional rules that allowed filling gaps back to 2006-07, which is particularly relevant for someone with a long self-employment history

💬 Response (15,658 in / 846 out)
Here is a clear plan for what you should do now, and what to do when you reach retirement.

## Step 1 — Check your National Insurance record now (most urgent)

This is the most important thing to do before you retire. As a self-employed person, you may have gaps in your record that reduce your State Pension.

1. Sign in to your Personal Tax Account at gov.uk/check-national-insurance-record to see your full NI record
2. Get a State Pension forecast at the same time — this shows exactly what you are on track to receive
3. If you have gaps, you may be able to pay voluntary Class 3 NI contributions to fill them

Note: there are strict deadlines for filling gaps. The extended window for tax years 2006-07 to 2016-17 has now closed. For most other years, you generally have 6 years to fill a gap. Act quickly.

More detail: [Voluntary National Insurance contributions on GOV.UK](https://www.gov.uk/voluntary-national-insurance-contributions)

## Step 2 — Claim your State Pension (up to 4 months before you turn 66)

The State Pension age is currently 66, not 65. You will need to wait until you turn 66 to receive it. It is not paid automatically — you must claim it.

- The full new State Pension is £221.20 per week (2024/25)
- You need 35 qualifying NI years for the full amount
- Apply up to 4 months before your 66th birthday at [gov.uk/new-state-pension](https://www.gov.uk/new-state-pension/eligibility)

## Step 3 — Trace any lost pensions

If you have had multiple jobs or pension schemes over your career, use the free [Pension Tracing Service](https://www.gov.uk/find-pension-contact-details) to track down any lost workplace or personal pensions.

## Step 4 — Check what else you may be entitled to at retirement

Once you are receiving your State Pension, also look into:

- Pension Credit — tops up your income if it is below £218.15/week (single person). This is a gateway benefit that unlocks other entitlements. Check at [gov.uk/pension-credit](https://www.gov.uk/pension-credit)
- Free bus pass — available from State Pension age via your local council at [gov.uk/apply-for-elderly-person-bus-pass](https://www.gov.uk/apply-for-elderly-person-bus-pass)
- Council Tax Reduction — if you are on a low income, apply to your local council
- Income tax — your State Pension counts as taxable income, so check your tax position at [gov.uk/tax-on-pension](https://www.gov.uk/tax-on-pension)

## Your most urgent action

Go to [gov.uk/check-national-insurance-record](https://www.gov.uk/check-national-insurance-record) today to check your NI record and get a State Pension forecast. This will tell you exactly where you stand and whether paying voluntary contributions would increase your pension.

💬 Response (12,992 in / 794 out)
Here is what you need to know to decide whether filling your gaps is worth it.

## The key question: how many qualifying years do you have now?

You need 35 qualifying years for the full new State Pension. If you already have 35 or more, filling gaps will not increase your pension at all. If you have fewer than 35, each extra year you add will increase what you get.

## What it costs to fill a gap

As a self-employed person, you would likely pay Class 2 or Class 3 voluntary contributions:

- Class 2 costs £3.50 a week, which is around £182 per year
- Class 3 costs £17.75 a week, which is around £923 per year

Class 2 is much cheaper and may apply to you if you were self-employed during the gap years. Your NI record online will tell you which class applies to each gap.

## Is it worth it?

The full new State Pension is currently £221.20 a week. Each qualifying year adds roughly one thirty-fifth of that, which is around £6.32 a week or £328 a year. At Class 2 rates, you could recover the cost of filling one year in under a year of retirement. At Class 3 rates, it takes around 3 years to break even.

However, filling gaps does not always increase your pension, for example if you were contracted out of the additional State Pension at any point. Your online forecast will flag this.

## What to do next

1. Check your State Pension forecast at [Check your State Pension on GOV.UK](https://www.gov.uk/check-state-pension) — it will tell you exactly how many qualifying years you have and whether paying more would help
2. Check which gaps are worth filling and what class of contribution applies at [Voluntary National Insurance on GOV.UK](https://www.gov.uk/voluntary-national-insurance-contributions)
3. Call the Future Pension Centre on 0800 731 0175 if you want to talk it through before paying — they can confirm whether each gap would actually increase your pension

The most important thing is not to pay anything until you have confirmed it will increase your pension. The online forecast and the Future Pension Centre can both tell you this for free.

💬 Response (7,275 in / 672 out)
For most people in your situation, filling gaps is very good value. Here is how the numbers work.

## The cost of filling a gap year

As a self-employed person, you would pay Class 2 or Class 3 voluntary contributions depending on your circumstances.

- Class 2 — £3.50 per week (£182 per year). You may qualify for this lower rate if you were self-employed during the gap year
- Class 3 — £17.75 per week (£923 per year). This is the standard rate if Class 2 does not apply

## What you get back

The full new State Pension is £230.25 per week (2025/26). Each qualifying year you add increases your pension by roughly £6.58 per week (£230.25 divided by 35 years).

So each gap year you fill adds around £342 per year to your State Pension.

At Class 2 rates, you recover the cost of filling a gap in under 7 months of retirement.
At Class 3 rates, you recover the cost in around 2 years and 8 months.

Given that the average retirement lasts 20 or more years, filling gaps is almost always worth it financially.

## Important things to check first

Before paying, you should confirm:

- Whether you can still fill each specific gap year — not all years are payable
- Whether filling a particular gap will actually increase your pension (your forecast will show this)
- Whether you will reach 35 qualifying years even without filling all 8 gaps — if so, extra years beyond 35 may not increase your pension at all

## What to do next

1. Call the Future Pension Centre on 0800 731 0175 — they can tell you exactly which gaps are worth filling and whether Class 2 rates apply to you
2. Check which years you can pay at [gov.uk/voluntary-national-insurance-contributions](https://www.gov.uk/voluntary-national-insurance-contributions)
3. Pay via HMRC — details at [gov.uk/pay-voluntary-class-3-national-insurance](https://www.gov.uk/pay-voluntary-class-3-national-insurance)

Call the Future Pension Centre first before paying anything — they will confirm which gaps are payable and which rate applies to you.

💬 Response (23,128 in / 942 out)
There are two separate things to look at here: your State Pension and bereavement benefits.

## Your State Pension

Because you were married before 6 April 2016, you may be able to inherit part of your husband's State Pension on top of your own. Specifically:

- If your husband reached State Pension age on or after 6 April 2016 and died on or after that date, you can inherit half of his "protected payment" — this is an extra amount some people built up under the old system
- If he reached State Pension age before 6 April 2016, you may inherit part of his Additional State Pension instead
- This inherited amount will be paid on top of your own State Pension when you reach State Pension age

Your own NI record still forms the basis of your State Pension — his record does not replace yours. This makes filling your own gaps, as discussed earlier, still very important.

You can read the full rules at [Inheriting State Pension from a spouse on GOV.UK](https://www.gov.uk/new-state-pension/inheriting-or-increasing-state-pension-from-a-spouse-or-civil-partner).

## Bereavement Support Payment

This is a separate benefit paid after a partner dies. However, there is an important deadline:

- You must normally claim within 21 months of your husband's death
- As he died two years ago, you may have passed this deadline
- You may still be able to claim if his cause of death was only recently confirmed — call the Bereavement Service helpline to check

You can read the eligibility rules at [Bereavement Support Payment on GOV.UK](https://www.gov.uk/bereavement-support-payment/eligibility).

## What to do next

1. Call the Future Pension Centre on 0800 731 0175 to ask specifically about inheriting from your husband's record
2. Call the Bereavement Service helpline on 0800 151 2012 to check whether you can still claim Bereavement Support Payment

Do both of these as soon as possible, as time limits may apply.

💬 Response (27,357 in / 770 out)
Yes, your husband's NI record can make a real difference to what you receive. Here is what you need to know.

## Inheriting part of his State Pension

Because you were married before 6 April 2016 and he died on or after that date, you may be able to inherit half of his "protected payment" — this is any amount his State Pension entitlement was above the full flat rate under the old system.

This extra amount will be added on top of your own State Pension when you claim it at 66.

The new State Pension is based on your own NI record, so filling your gaps (as discussed) still matters. But the inherited protected payment is on top of that.

## What to check

- Whether your husband had a protected payment — his State Pension forecast would have shown this. You can contact the Pension Service to find out
- You will not be able to inherit anything if you remarry or form a new civil partnership before you reach State Pension age (66)

## Council Tax single person discount

If you now live alone, you are entitled to a 25% reduction on your Council Tax bill. Contact your local council to apply if you have not already done so at [gov.uk/council-tax-discounts](https://www.gov.uk/council-tax-discounts)

## Bereavement Support Payment — check if you claimed

This is a lump sum plus monthly payments for people widowed under State Pension age. You had up to 21 months from the date of death to claim. If your husband died two years ago, this window may have closed — but it is worth checking with DWP on 0800 731 0469 in case any entitlement remains.

## What to do next

Call the Future Pension Centre on 0800 731 0175 and tell them you are widowed. They can confirm whether your husband had a protected payment and exactly how much you will inherit when you claim your State Pension.

Full details are at [gov.uk/new-state-pension/inheriting-or-increasing-state-pension-from-a-spouse-or-civil-partner](https://www.gov.uk/new-state-pension/inheriting-or-increasing-state-pension-from-a-spouse-or-civil-partner)

Here is a clear order of what you need to do. Because your mother owned her home, there are some extra steps around probate and the property.

## Step 1 - Register the death

You must do this within 5 days (8 days in Scotland). Contact the local register office where she died. You will get a death certificate, which you will need for almost everything else.

[Register a death on GOV.UK](https://www.gov.uk/register-a-death)

## Step 2 - Use Tell Us Once

After registering, the register office will give you a Tell Us Once reference number. Use it to notify multiple government departments in one go — including HMRC, DWP, the Passport Office, and the DVLA.

[Use the Tell Us Once service](https://www.gov.uk/after-a-death/organisations-you-need-to-contact-and-tell-us-once)

## Step 3 - Arrange the funeral

You can do this at any point after registering the death.

[Get help paying for a funeral](https://www.gov.uk/funeral-payments) if you need financial support.

## Step 4 - Value the estate and check Inheritance Tax

Because your mother owned her home, you need to work out the value of everything she owned — property, savings, and possessions. This tells you whether Inheritance Tax is due. The threshold is currently £325,000, though homeowners may qualify for a higher threshold.

You must report the estate's value before you can apply for probate.

[How to value an estate for Inheritance Tax](https://www.gov.uk/valuing-estate-of-someone-who-died)

## Step 5 - Apply for probate

Because your mother owned property in her name alone, you will almost certainly need probate. This gives you the legal right to deal with her estate, including selling or transferring the home.

- If she left a will, the executor named in it applies
- If there is no will, the closest living relative can apply

[Apply for probate on GOV.UK](https://www.gov.uk/applying-for-probate)

## Step 6 - Deal with the estate

Once probate is granted, you can:

- pay any debts she owed
- close bank accounts
- sell or transfer the property
- distribute what remains according to the will or the law

[Guidance on dealing with the estate](https://www.gov.uk/wills-probate-inheritance/dealing-with-the-estate)

## Also notify privately

As well as government, you will need to contact her bank, utility companies, landlord (if applicable), and any private pension providers directly.

---

Start by [registering the death at your local register office](https://www.gov.uk/register-a-death) — everything else follows from that. The full step-by-step guide is at [What to do when someone dies](https://www.gov.uk/when-someone-dies).

💬 Response (10,924 in / 754 out)
Here is a step-by-step guide to what you need to do. Because your mother owned her home, there are some important legal and financial steps on top of the usual ones.

## Phase 1 - Do these first

1. Register the death at your local register office within 5 days. You will need a medical certificate of cause of death from a doctor or coroner. See: gov.uk/register-a-death

2. Order several certified copies of the death certificate at the same time. Banks, insurers and probate all need original copies. They cost £11 each. See: gov.uk/order-copy-birth-death-marriage-certificate

3. Use Tell Us Once after you register. This notifies up to 23 government departments (HMRC, DVLA, DWP, passport office, your mother's local council) in one go. The register office will give you a reference number to use it. See: gov.uk/after-a-death/organisations-you-need-to-contact-and-tell-us-once

4. Check if Inheritance Tax is due. Because your mother owned her home, her estate may exceed the £325,000 nil-rate band (or £500,000 if the home passes to a direct descendant). If it does, you must complete form IHT400 and pay any tax within 6 months of the date of death — before probate is granted. See: gov.uk/inheritance-tax

5. If your mother had a Lasting Power of Attorney in place, notify the Office of the Public Guardian that she has died and return the LPA document. It automatically stops being valid on death. See: gov.uk/power-of-attorney/end-a-poa

## Phase 2 - Do this once the above are in hand

6. Apply for a Grant of Probate. Because your mother owned property, you will almost certainly need this to transfer or sell the home. It gives you legal authority to deal with her estate. The fee is £273 and processing currently takes 16 weeks or more. See: gov.uk/applying-for-probate

## Other things to check

- Funeral Expenses Payment - if you are on a qualifying benefit such as Universal Credit or Pension Credit, you may be able to get help with funeral costs. You must claim within 6 months of the funeral. See: gov.uk/funeral-payments

- Council Tax - if your mother's property is now empty, contact her local council. An empty property may qualify for a discount or exemption while the estate is being dealt with.

- Cancel your mother's driving licence - Tell Us Once usually handles this, but if you have the licence, send it to the DVLA directly.

The most urgent step is to register the death within 5 days. Start there, and everything else follows from that.

💬 Response (29,669 in / 1,060 out)
Yes, this does change things — in two important ways: Inheritance Tax and the Spanish property process.

## Inheritance Tax — overseas assets must be included

If your mother lived in the UK (was "domiciled" here), HMRC requires you to include all her worldwide assets in the estate valuation — including the Spanish property and all savings. This makes it more likely the estate will need a full Inheritance Tax return using form IHT400, rather than the simpler process.

You will also need to complete a supplementary form called IHT417 (Foreign Assets), which covers property and assets held outside the UK.

- include the Spanish property's market value in the estate total
- get a professional valuation of the Spanish property in pounds sterling
- report everything to HMRC using [form IHT400 and its supplementary forms](https://www.gov.uk/government/publications/inheritance-tax-inheritance-tax-account-iht400)

## The Spanish property — a separate legal process

Dealing with property in Spain is not covered by UK probate. Spanish law applies to property in Spain, and you will need to go through a separate legal process there.

You will likely need to:

1. Instruct a solicitor or notary in Spain who specialises in inheritance law
2. Get the UK grant of probate officially recognised in Spain (this is called "apostille" recognition)
3. Pay any Spanish inheritance tax that may be due — Spain has its own rules, and rates vary by region
4. Transfer or sell the property under Spanish law

## Strongly recommended — get professional help

Because this estate involves overseas property, it is more complex than most. A solicitor experienced in cross-border estates can help you avoid mistakes that could be costly or cause delays.

- [Find a solicitor via the Law Society](https://www.gov.uk/find-a-legal-adviser)
- [Value the estate and report to HMRC](https://www.gov.uk/valuing-estate-of-someone-who-died)

GOV.UK does not provide detailed guidance on Spanish inheritance law specifically, so for the Spain side you will need advice from a Spanish lawyer or a UK solicitor with international expertise.
⚖️ Judge scores — Completeness: 4/5 · Accuracy: 4/5 · Sequencing: 4/5 · Clarity: 5/5
Completeness 4/5 ●●●●○
The response covers the conditional service (IHT400) well and mentions apostille/legalisation for the death certificate, though it doesn't explicitly name the FCO document legalisation service (fco-document-legalisation) as a distinct step — it references 'apostille recognition' but doesn't direct the citizen to the specific GOV.UK legalisation service page.
Accuracy 4/5 ●●●●○
The information about IHT400, IHT417, worldwide assets, and the need for apostille is broadly correct; the minor imprecision is describing apostille as 'recognition' of the grant of probate rather than clearly noting that the death certificate itself needs legalisation/apostille for foreign use, and the process is via the FCO legalisation office.
Sequencing 4/5 ●●●●○
The response logically orders UK tax obligations before the Spanish property process, and within the Spanish section correctly places obtaining UK probate before Spanish recognition, though it could more explicitly sequence getting the death certificate apostilled as an early step.
Clarity 5/5 ●●●●●
The response is exceptionally well-structured with clear headings, bullet points, numbered steps, and relevant links, making it very easy for a citizen to follow.
Notable omissions: fco-document-legalisation
Notable errors: Apostille described as 'recognition' of grant of probate rather than clearly identifying that the death certificate needs legalisation via the FCO legalisation service for use in Spain

No tool calls this turn
💬 Response (2,274 in / 434 out)
Yes, it does add some steps. The savings are straightforward, but the Spanish property makes things more complex.

## Savings

- You will still need the Grant of Probate (step 6 above) before most UK banks will release funds
- Contact each bank or building society directly with a certified copy of the death certificate to freeze the accounts and find out their process
- If savings are in an ISA, the value is frozen at the date of death but can be transferred to a spouse — this does not apply here as she lived alone, so the ISA forms part of the estate

## Spanish Property

This is the most significant change. Owning property abroad means you need to deal with two legal systems at the same time.

- Spanish inheritance law applies to the property in Spain, regardless of what a UK will says
- You will need a Spanish lawyer (known as a "notario" or solicitor with Spanish law expertise) to handle the transfer or sale of the property
- You will need an Apostille-stamped copy of the UK death certificate, translated into Spanish by a certified translator
- The Spanish property must still be declared on the UK Inheritance Tax return (form IHT400), even though Spain may also charge its own inheritance tax
- Spain has its own inheritance tax rules which vary by region — a Spanish lawyer can advise on this
- You may be able to offset Spanish inheritance tax paid against the UK liability to avoid being taxed twice, under double taxation rules

## What to do next

- Get a Spanish probate lawyer as soon as possible — delays can lead to penalties in Spain
- The deadline to accept or renounce a Spanish inheritance is generally 6 months from the date of death
- Contact HMRC if you are unsure how to declare the Spanish property on the UK Inheritance Tax return: gov.uk/inheritance-tax/overseas-property-and-assets

The most urgent new step is to instruct a Spanish lawyer this week, alongside registering the death in the UK.
⚖️ Judge scores — Completeness: 4/5 · Accuracy: 3/5 · Sequencing: 4/5 · Clarity: 5/5
Completeness 4/5 ●●●●○
Covers the essential FCO document legalisation (apostille) and the conditional IHT400 requirement for declaring overseas property, though the specific FCO legalisation service is not named explicitly by its gov.uk service name.
Accuracy 3/5 ●●●○○
The response conflates a notario (a Spanish public official, not a lawyer) with a solicitor, which is a meaningful error; also the statement that 'Spanish inheritance law applies to the property in Spain, regardless of what a UK will says' is an oversimplification given EU Succession Regulation 650/2012 which allows choice of law of nationality, though the UK is not bound by it Spain is.
Sequencing 4/5 ●●●●○
The advice to instruct a Spanish lawyer urgently alongside UK death registration is well-prioritised, and the 6-month Spanish deadline is correctly flagged; minor imprecision in not explicitly sequencing the apostille step before the Spanish proceedings.
Clarity 5/5 ●●●●●
Excellently structured with clear headings for savings, Spanish property, and next steps; appropriate depth and actionable guidance throughout.
Notable omissions: fco-document-legalisation (not named explicitly as a gov.uk service)
Notable errors: Notario described as equivalent to a lawyer/solicitor when it is a distinct public official role in Spain, Oversimplification of which inheritance law applies to Spanish property

💬 Response (38,896 in / 1,046 out)
Here is what you need to know about your mother's Pension Credit.

## Pension Credit stops on death

Pension Credit is a personal benefit. It stops when the claimant dies. Any payments made after the date of death are classed as overpayments and must be returned to DWP.

## What you need to do

1. Use the Tell Us Once service (which you will get when you register the death) — this automatically notifies DWP, including the Pension Credit team
2. If any payments have gone into her bank account since she died, do not spend them — DWP will ask for them back
3. If you cannot use Tell Us Once, contact the Pension Credit helpline directly on 0800 99 1234

## Other benefits she may have received

Because she was on Pension Credit, she may also have been getting linked benefits such as:

- Housing Benefit (if applicable — though she owned her home, this is unlikely)
- Council Tax Reduction — notify her local council directly
- Cold Weather Payments or Warm Home Discount — these stop automatically
- Free TV licence (if aged 75 or over) — notify TV Licensing directly

## Watch out for overpayments

DWP may write to you as the person dealing with the estate about any money owed. This would be treated as a debt of the estate, to be settled before assets are distributed.

You can find more detail at [what to do after someone dies on GOV.UK](https://www.gov.uk/after-a-death) and the [Tell Us Once service](https://www.gov.uk/after-a-death/organisations-you-need-to-contact-and-tell-us-once).

Use Tell Us Once as soon as you have registered the death — it is the quickest way to stop payments and avoid a larger overpayment building up.

The response correctly covers Pension Credit cessation and Tell Us Once but omits Bereavement Support Payment (an essential service per the checklist) and Funeral Expenses Payment (a conditional service, highly relevant given Pension Credit eligibility).

The information about Pension Credit stopping, overpayments, Tell Us Once, and linked benefits is broadly accurate, though the parenthetical about home ownership appears to be fabricated context not provided by the citizen.

Clarity 5/5 ●●●●●
The response is exceptionally well-structured with clear headings, numbered steps, bullet points, and actionable links, making it very easy to follow.
Notable omissions: dwp-bereavement-support, dwp-funeral-payment
Notable errors: Assumes the mother owned her home, which is not stated in the citizen's prompt

💬 Response (28,372 in / 736 out)
Here is what happens to your mother's Pension Credit and what you need to do.

## Pension Credit stops on death

Pension Credit is a personal benefit. It stops on the date of death and cannot be transferred to anyone else. You need to notify the Pension Credit service directly.

## How to report the death

The good news is that Tell Us Once (which you use after registering the death) will notify DWP automatically. This should stop the Pension Credit payments.

However, it is also worth calling the Pension Credit helpline directly to make sure payments stop promptly and to ask about any overpayments.

- Pension Credit helpline: 0800 99 1234 (Monday to Friday, 8am to 6pm)
- Have your mother's National Insurance number ready when you call

## Overpayments

- If any Pension Credit payments were made after the date of death, DWP will ask for these to be paid back from the estate
- Do not spend any money from your mother's accounts until you have confirmed with DWP that no overpayment is owed

## State Pension

- Your mother's State Pension also stops on the date of death
- Tell Us Once handles this notification too, but again you can call the Pension Service on 0800 731 0469 to confirm

## Other benefits linked to Pension Credit

Because your mother received Pension Credit, she may also have been getting:

- Council Tax Reduction — her local council needs to be told separately if Tell Us Once does not cover this
- Cold Weather Payments or Warm Home Discount — these stop automatically
- Free TV licence (if she was 75 or over) — notify TV Licensing directly

See: gov.uk/after-a-death/organisations-you-need-to-contact-and-tell-us-once

The most important step is to use Tell Us Once as soon as you have registered the death, then call the Pension Credit helpline to confirm everything has been stopped.

💬 Response (14,992 in / 999 out)
Here is what you should do, in order of priority.

## Check your redundancy pay

After five years with your employer, you are entitled to statutory redundancy pay. The amount depends on your age and weekly wage. Use the [statutory redundancy pay calculator on GOV.UK](https://www.gov.uk/calculate-your-redundancy-pay) to find out what you are owed. Your employer must pay this to you.

You can read more about [your rights during redundancy](https://www.gov.uk/redundancy-your-rights) including notice periods and what to do if your employer does not pay.

## Claim Universal Credit straight away

You can claim Universal Credit to help cover your living costs while you look for work. Claim as soon as possible — payments are made monthly and there is usually a wait before your first payment arrives.

Go to [Universal Credit on GOV.UK](https://www.gov.uk/universal-credit) to check eligibility and apply.

## Contact your mortgage lender

Speak to your lender now, before you miss any payments. Many lenders offer a payment holiday or reduced payments for people who have lost their job.

## Apply for Support for Mortgage Interest

Once you are receiving Universal Credit, you may be able to get [Support for Mortgage Interest (SMI)](https://www.gov.uk/support-for-mortgage-interest). This is a government loan that helps cover the interest part of your mortgage. You repay it when you sell your home.

## Steps to take now

1. Use the [redundancy pay calculator](https://www.gov.uk/calculate-your-redundancy-pay) to check what you are owed
2. [Claim Universal Credit](https://www.gov.uk/universal-credit) today — do not delay as this affects when you get paid
3. Call your mortgage lender to explain your situation
4. Once your Universal Credit claim is in place, ask about [SMI](https://www.gov.uk/support-for-mortgage-interest)
5. Contact [Citizens Advice](https://www.citizensadvice.org.uk) if you need free help managing debts

Start your Universal Credit claim today, as the sooner you apply, the sooner your payments can begin.

💬 Response (17,037 in / 982 out)
Here is a clear plan for your situation. You have five years of service, so you have important rights and several services available to you right now.

## Step 1 — Get your P45 and redundancy pay

These two things come first, as you need them for everything else.

- Ask your employer for your P45 on or before your last working day — they are legally required to give it to you
- You are entitled to statutory redundancy pay after two or more years of service — your employer must pay this; if they refuse, you can take them to an Employment Tribunal
- Check your redundancy pay amount using the GOV.UK redundancy pay calculator at https://www.gov.uk/calculate-your-redundancy-pay

## Step 2 — Claim benefits straight away

Do not delay — some payments take several weeks to come through.

- Apply for Universal Credit at https://www.gov.uk/universal-credit — this is the main benefit for people out of work; report your change of circumstances within one month
- Apply for New Style Jobseeker's Allowance at https://www.gov.uk/jobseekers-allowance/new-style-jsa — you can claim this alongside Universal Credit if you have paid enough National Insurance in the last two tax years; it is paid for up to six months
- When you claim Universal Credit, National Insurance credits are awarded automatically, which protects your State Pension record while you are not working

## Step 3 — Protect your mortgage

Because you have a mortgage and no savings, this is urgent.

- Contact your mortgage lender as soon as possible and ask about a mortgage payment holiday or reduced payments — lenders must consider this
- Apply for Support for Mortgage Interest at https://www.gov.uk/support-for-mortgage-interest — this is a government loan to help pay the interest on your mortgage while you are on Universal Credit or JSA; it is secured against your property and repaid when you sell
- Apply for Council Tax Reduction through your local council at https://www.gov.uk/apply-council-tax-reduction — this can reduce your bill significantly or to zero

## Step 4 — Reduce other costs

- Apply for the NHS Low Income Scheme (HC2 certificate) at https://www.gov.uk/nhs-low-income-scheme — this can give you free or reduced-cost prescriptions, dental treatment and sight tests
- Ask your broadband provider about a social tariff — discounted packages (typically £12–20 per month) are available to Universal Credit claimants; see https://www.gov.uk/affordable-broadband
- Claim an income tax refund if you have overpaid PAYE this tax year and are not returning to work before 5 April — use form P50 at https://www.gov.uk/claim-tax-refund

## Step 5 — If you are worried about debt

- Contact a free debt advice service such as StepChange (stepchange.org) or Citizens Advice (citizensadvice.org.uk) straight away
- You may be able to apply for Breathing Space at https://www.gov.uk/options-for-dealing-with-your-debts/breathing-space — this gives you 60 days of legal protection from creditor action while you get debt advice

## Step 6 — Start looking for work

- Use the free DWP job search service at https://www.gov.uk/find-a-job

## If your redundancy was unfair

- Contact Acas for free advice and early conciliation at https://www.gov.uk/contact-acas — you must do this before making an Employment Tribunal claim
- The time limit for most tribunal claims is three months minus one day from the date of dismissal, so do not wait

Your most urgent action is to apply for Universal Credit and contact your mortgage lender today.

💬 Response (13,724 in / 716 out)
Yes, being signed off does affect things in a few important ways.

## Statutory Sick Pay

You cannot get Statutory Sick Pay (SSP) from your previous employer because you have already been made redundant and are no longer an employee. SSP is only paid by an employer to a current employee.

## Universal Credit

Being signed off with a health condition means you may get extra money through Universal Credit. You will need to report your health condition when you apply. The DWP may then ask you to have a Work Capability Assessment to decide how much your condition limits your ability to work.

If they find you have limited capability for work, you will not be required to look for jobs while you are unwell, and you may receive a higher payment. You can read more about [health conditions and Universal Credit on GOV.UK](https://www.gov.uk/health-conditions-disability-universal-credit).

## What to do now

1. When you apply for Universal Credit, tell them straight away that you have been signed off by your GP
2. Upload or post your fit note to your Universal Credit account as soon as you have it
3. Do not wait until you feel better to apply — claim now and report your condition as part of the process

## New Style Employment and Support Allowance

You may also be able to claim [New Style Employment and Support Allowance (ESA)](https://www.gov.uk/employment-support-allowance) alongside Universal Credit. This is based on your National Insurance contributions. After five years of employment, you are likely to qualify.

Start your Universal Credit claim today and make sure you report your health condition during the application.

💬 Response (8,731 in / 647 out)
Yes, being signed off with stress and anxiety changes a few things. Here is what you need to know.

## New Style Employment and Support Allowance (ESA)

Instead of New Style Jobseeker's Allowance, you should now claim New Style ESA. This is the benefit for people whose health condition prevents or limits their ability to work.

- You need your fit note from your GP to claim — keep all fit notes safe
- You must have paid enough National Insurance in the last two tax years, which you likely have after five years of employment
- DWP will arrange a Work Capability Assessment to decide how your condition affects your ability to work
- The weekly rate is £92.05 if you are 25 or over
- You can still claim Universal Credit alongside New Style ESA
- Call the ESA helpline to start your claim: 0800 055 6688 (Monday to Friday, 8am to 6pm)
- Full details at https://www.gov.uk/employment-support-allowance

## Universal Credit health element

Once you are on Universal Credit, tell them about your fit note. If your Work Capability Assessment finds you have limited capability for work, you may receive a higher amount of Universal Credit on top of the standard payment.

## Personal Independence Payment (PIP)

If your stress and anxiety significantly affects your daily life — for example, managing medication, making decisions, or leaving the house — you may be able to claim PIP. It is not means-tested, so your income and savings do not affect it.

- You must have had difficulties for at least 3 months and expect them to last at least 9 months
- It is worth starting a claim now if your condition is ongoing, as assessments can take several months
- Call the PIP helpline: 0800 917 2222 (Monday to Friday, 8am to 5pm)
- Full details at https://www.gov.uk/pip

## What to do now

1. Contact your GP to make sure you have a current fit note
2. Call the ESA helpline today to start your ESA claim
3. Report your health condition when you apply for or update your Universal Credit claim
4. Consider starting a PIP claim if your condition affects your daily life

Your most urgent step is to call the ESA helpline today, as payments can take several weeks to begin.

💬 Response (23,096 in / 861 out)
Here is how your £12,000 redundancy payment affects things.

## Universal Credit

Your £12,000 sits between two key thresholds:

- Under £6,000 — no effect on your payments
- Between £6,000 and £16,000 — you can still claim, but your payments are reduced
- Over £16,000 — you cannot claim at all

Because you have £12,000, you can still claim Universal Credit, but your payments will be reduced. The DWP treats every £250 over £6,000 as if it earns you £4.35 per month in income, even if it does not. This is called "tariff income." So on £12,000, that is roughly £104 per month taken off your Universal Credit payment.

As you spend the money on living costs, your payments will gradually increase. You must report your savings balance honestly and update it when it changes.

## New Style ESA

Your redundancy payment does not affect New Style ESA at all. Savings and capital are not taken into account for this benefit. This is another reason it is worth claiming New Style ESA alongside Universal Credit.

## Important things to know

- You must declare the £12,000 when you apply for Universal Credit
- The value of your home is not counted as savings
- Do not deliberately spend the money quickly just to reduce your savings — the DWP can treat this as "deprivation of capital" and still count it

## What to do now

1. Claim [New Style ESA](https://www.gov.uk/employment-support-allowance) first, as your savings do not affect it
2. Then claim [Universal Credit](https://www.gov.uk/universal-credit) and declare your redundancy payment honestly
3. Use a [benefits calculator](https://www.gov.uk/benefits-calculators) to get an estimate of what you will receive

You can find full details on the [Universal Credit savings and capital rules on GOV.UK](https://www.gov.uk/guidance/universal-credit-money-savings-and-investments).

💬 Response (3,064 in / 524 out)
Yes, your redundancy payment does affect some benefits but not others. Here is a clear breakdown.

## Benefits it does NOT affect

- New Style ESA — your redundancy payment does not affect this at all; it is based on your National Insurance record only
- New Style JSA — same as above, not affected
- PIP — not means-tested, so your redundancy payment makes no difference

## Universal Credit — it depends on the amount

Universal Credit is means-tested, so your redundancy payment counts as capital (savings).

- If your capital is under £6,000, it does not affect your Universal Credit payments at all
- If your capital is between £6,000 and £16,000, your Universal Credit is reduced — for every £250 (or part of £250) over £6,000, £4.35 is deducted from your monthly payment
- If your capital goes over £16,000, you cannot claim Universal Credit at all until it drops below that level

Your £12,000 redundancy payment puts you in the middle band right now. This means your Universal Credit will be reduced, but you can still claim it.

## Important — how DWP treats redundancy pay

- DWP expects you to use the money for living costs, not to put it aside
- If DWP believes you have deliberately reduced your capital to get more benefit, they can treat you as still having that money — this is called "deprivation of capital"
- Keep records of everything you spend the money on

## What this means in practice

Your capital calculation works roughly like this:

- £12,000 minus £6,000 = £6,000 excess capital
- £6,000 divided by £250 = 24 units
- 24 multiplied by £4.35 = £104.40 deducted from your monthly Universal Credit

As you spend the money on normal living costs and your mortgage, your capital will fall and your Universal Credit payments will increase accordingly. Report any changes in your capital to DWP each month.

You can check current Universal Credit rates at https://www.gov.uk/universal-credit/what-youll-get.

Your next step is to apply for Universal Credit now and declare your £12,000 as capital when you do — do not delay, as the claim start date affects when you get paid.
⚖️ Judge scores — Completeness: 5/5 · Accuracy: 4/5 · Sequencing: 4/5 · Clarity: 5/5
Completeness 5/5 ●●●●●
Covers the essential UC service with detailed capital rules, correctly identifies New Style JSA as unaffected by capital, and proactively mentions New Style ESA and PIP — going beyond what the citizen asked.
Accuracy 4/5 ●●●●○
The UC capital bands and tariff income mechanism are correctly described; the £4.35 per £250 figure is the current rate and broadly correct, though minor imprecision exists — the statement that 'DWP expects you to use the money for living costs, not to put it aside' is an oversimplification of deprivation of capital rules, which focus on deliberate disposal to increase benefit entitlement rather than a general expectation to spend savings.
Sequencing 4/5 ●●●●○
The response sensibly advises applying for UC immediately and declaring capital upfront, and explains the trajectory of reducing capital over time, though it could have more explicitly noted that New Style JSA should be considered as a parallel claim since it is not capital-tested.
Clarity 5/5 ●●●●●
Exceptionally well-structured with clear headings, a worked calculation example, and actionable next steps; the information is appropriately scoped and easy to follow for a lay citizen.
Notable errors: The deprivation of capital explanation slightly overstates DWP's expectations — spending savings on normal living costs is fine and does not constitute deprivation; the framing could cause unnecessary anxiety.

💬 Response (33,138 in / 1,405 out)
Here is a full breakdown of what you need to do, in order.

## 1. Register your limited company with Companies House

This is the first step — you cannot trade as a limited company until you do this.

1. Verify your identity with Companies House (required for all directors)
2. Choose a company name, appoint at least one director, and issue shares
3. Register online via [set up a private limited company on GOV.UK](https://www.gov.uk/limited-company-formation) — it costs £50 and is usually done within 24 hours

## 2. Register for Corporation Tax with HMRC

You must do this within 3 months of starting to trade. HMRC will send you a letter with your Unique Taxpayer Reference (UTR) after you register with Companies House. Use your UTR to [register for Corporation Tax via HMRC online services](https://www.gov.uk/log-in-register-hmrc-online-services).

## 3. Register for the Construction Industry Scheme (CIS) as a contractor

Because you will be paying subcontractors for construction work, you must [register as a CIS contractor with HMRC](https://www.gov.uk/what-you-must-do-as-a-cis-contractor). This means you will need to deduct money from subcontractor payments and pass it to HMRC each month. You can do this through the same HMRC online services account.

## 4. Register as an employer and set up PAYE

Even if you are the only director paying yourself, you must [register as an employer with HMRC](https://www.gov.uk/register-employer) before your first payday. You cannot register more than 2 months before you start paying people.

## 5. Register for VAT

You must register for VAT if your taxable turnover goes over £90,000 in any 12-month period. For a building business, it is often worth registering voluntarily before that, so you can reclaim VAT on materials and equipment. You can [register for VAT through HMRC online services](https://www.gov.uk/vat-registration).

## 6. Get the right insurance

As an employer, you are legally required to have Employers Liability insurance. You should also consider public liability insurance, which is standard in the building trade. GOV.UK has guidance on [business insurance requirements](https://www.gov.uk/employers-liability-insurance).

## 7. Check if you need any licences or permits

Depending on the type of work you do, you may need specific licences — for example, for working with gas, electrics, or asbestos. Check the [GOV.UK licence finder](https://www.gov.uk/licence-finder) to see what applies to your business.

---

Start by registering your company at [gov.uk/limited-company-formation](https://www.gov.uk/limited-company-formation) — everything else follows from that.

💬 Response (15,007 in / 1,214 out)
Here is a full guide to setting up your limited company building and renovation business in London.

## Step 1 — Register your company

1. Check your chosen company name is available on the Companies House register
2. Register your limited company online using form IN01 — costs £50 and can be done the same day
3. You must appoint at least one director

GOV.UK: [Register a limited company](https://www.gov.uk/limited-company-formation/register-your-company)

## Step 2 — Register for taxes (deadlines apply)

Do these after you have registered your company:

- Register for Corporation Tax within 3 months of starting to trade — this is a legal requirement for all limited companies
- Register for Self Assessment by 5 October in your second year of trading — required for company directors
- Register for PAYE before your first payday if you plan to employ anyone
- Register for VAT if your turnover will exceed £90,000 in any 12-month period — you can also register voluntarily below this threshold to reclaim VAT on purchases

GOV.UK links: [Corporation Tax](https://www.gov.uk/register-for-corporation-tax) | [Self Assessment](https://www.gov.uk/register-for-self-assessment) | [PAYE](https://www.gov.uk/register-employer) | [VAT](https://www.gov.uk/vat-registration)

## Step 3 — Construction Industry Scheme (CIS)

This is specific to your trade and very important:

- If you pay subcontractors for construction work, you must register as a contractor with HMRC before making your first payment
- Subcontractors you hire should also register — this reduces the tax deduction rate from 30% to 20%

GOV.UK: [Construction Industry Scheme](https://www.gov.uk/what-is-the-construction-industry-scheme)

## Step 4 — Licences and permits for building work in London

As a building and renovation business, you will regularly need these from your local council (in London, this is your borough council):

- Road occupation licence — needed before you dig up or place equipment on a public road; apply directly to the council (cannot be done online)
- Skip permit — needed before placing a skip on a public road; your skip hire company can often get this for you, but confirm with the council

GOV.UK links: [Road occupation licence](https://www.gov.uk/find-licences/road-occupation-licence-for-building-work) | [Skip permit](https://www.gov.uk/find-licences/apply-skip-permit)

## Step 5 — Waste carrier registration

Building work produces waste. You must register with the Environment Agency before transporting or disposing of it:

- Lower-tier registration (free) — covers your own waste only
- Upper-tier registration (£184) — needed if you handle or arrange disposal of other people's waste

GOV.UK: [Waste carrier registration](https://www.gov.uk/register-renew-waste-carrier-broker-dealer-england)

## Step 6 — If you employ staff

- Get Employers' Liability Insurance before your first employee starts — you must have at least £5 million cover and display the certificate at your workplace
- Set up a workplace pension scheme before your first payday for any eligible employee aged 22 to State Pension age earning £10,000 or more per year

GOV.UK links: [Employers' Liability Insurance](https://www.gov.uk/employers-liability-insurance) | [Workplace pension](https://www.gov.uk/workplace-pensions-employers)

## Step 7 — Ongoing Companies House obligations

Once registered, you must do these every year:

- File annual accounts within 9 months of your financial year end
- File a confirmation statement within 14 days of your review period end (£34 fee)

GOV.UK links: [Annual accounts](https://www.gov.uk/file-your-company-annual-accounts) | [Confirmation statement](https://www.gov.uk/confirmation-statement)

## Also worth knowing

- If you use business premises (such as a yard or office), you may need to register for business rates with your London borough council — Small Business Rate Relief can eliminate the charge for properties with a rateable value under £12,000
- Search for grants and funding available to small businesses at [GOV.UK business finance support](https://www.gov.uk/business-finance-support)

---

Your most urgent first step is to [register your limited company at Companies House](https://www.gov.uk/limited-company-formation/register-your-company) — everything else follows from that. Let me know if you want more detail on any of these steps.

💬 Response (33,124 in / 1,018 out)
Yes, there are specific rules you need to follow. Here is what you need to know.

## Getting a skip permit

You cannot place a skip on a public road or footpath without a permit. Because you are operating in London, you need to contact the relevant London borough council for each area where you plan to place a skip. Each council sets its own fees.

Before you can apply for a permit, your council may require you to register as a skip operator. To do that you will need:

- proof of public liability insurance with at least £5,000,000 cover
- a copy of a waste transfer note
- a waste carrier's licence
- a goods vehicle operator's licence

## Conditions you must meet

Once you have a permit, the skip must be:

- marked with your company name, address and phone number
- painted yellow on both ends
- in good condition with no holes
- not blocking drains, roads, footpaths or verges
- not causing a hazard to pedestrians or other road users

The council will tell you how long the skip can stay in place.

## Fines

If you place an unlicensed skip on a public road, cause damage to public property, or fail to keep the skip safe, the council can fine you.

Use the [skip operator licence page on GOV.UK](https://www.gov.uk/skip-operator-licence) to find your local London borough council and apply.

💬 Response (7,440 in / 407 out)
Here is what you need to know about placing skips on public roads.

## Who applies

In most areas, the skip hire company applies for the permit on your behalf. In some London boroughs, you may need to apply yourself. Check with your skip hire company first — and confirm with your local borough council either way.

## What requires a permit

You need a permit for:

- standard skips
- hippobags and other skip bags

You do not need a permit if the skip is placed entirely on private land (for example, a driveway or private car park).

## Safety requirements

When a skip is on a public road, you must display:

- reflective markings on the skip
- traffic cones around it
- safety lamps at night
- the skip hire company's name and telephone number

The exact requirements vary by London borough, so check with your local council.

## Fines

If you fail to display the required lights and markings, you can be fined up to £1,000.

## How to apply

Contact your local London borough council directly. You can find yours at [GOV.UK — find your local council](https://www.gov.uk/find-local-council).

Full details are on the [skip licence page on GOV.UK](https://www.gov.uk/find-licences/apply-skip-permit).

💬 Response (26,262 in / 830 out)
GOV.UK does not list many grants specifically for new construction businesses, but there are some useful options worth knowing about.

## Government-backed Start Up Loan

This is the main government scheme for new businesses. You can borrow between £500 and £25,000 at a fixed interest rate of 6% per year. It is a personal loan, not a grant, so you do need to pay it back over 1 to 5 years. You also get free mentoring for up to 12 months.

To be eligible you must:

- live in the UK
- be 18 or over
- have a business that has been trading for less than 36 months

You can [apply for a Start Up Loan on GOV.UK](https://www.gov.uk/apply-start-up-loan).

## Finance and support finder

GOV.UK has a tool that lists over 125 funding schemes filtered by industry, region, business stage and type of support. You can filter by construction and London to find schemes relevant to you. Use the [finance and support finder on GOV.UK](https://www.gov.uk/business-finance-support) to search.

## What to be aware of

There are very few outright grants available for new private construction businesses. Most government support comes in the form of loans, advice, or mentoring. Grants tend to be targeted at specific sectors such as green energy, research, or social housing.

Start with the [finance and support finder](https://www.gov.uk/business-finance-support) to see the full list of schemes available to you in London.

💬 Response (12,983 in / 574 out)
There are 25 schemes available for construction businesses in London. Here are the most relevant ones for a new business like yours.

## Loans and finance

- Start Up Loan — a government-backed personal loan of up to £25,000 at a fixed 6% interest rate, plus free mentoring; apply at [GOV.UK Start Up Loans](https://www.gov.uk/government/collections/start-up-loans-company)
- Funding Circle — peer-to-peer loans for small businesses once you are established
- Better Business Finance — a free directory of approved finance suppliers to help you find the right lender

## Free advice and support

- Business and Intellectual Property Centre (British Library, London) — free advice, workshops and access to business databases for new businesses
- Business advice and training (East of England and London) — free advice, workshops and loans for businesses trading less than 2 years
- Be the Business — free support to improve productivity for small businesses

## Winning contracts

- CompeteFor — free service to help small businesses compete for major public and private sector contracts, which could include public building projects
- Fit To Bid — free workshops and one-to-one advice to help you bid for contracts with large construction firms

## Training

- Help to Grow: Management — subsidised training for small business leaders to grow and improve their business

## How to search for more

You can filter all 25 schemes by type of support (grant, loan, advice) at [GOV.UK business finance support — construction in London](https://www.gov.uk/business-finance-support?business_stage[]=start-ups&industries[]=construction&regions[]=london).

A good first step is to apply for a Start Up Loan or visit the Business and Intellectual Property Centre at the British Library — both are free to access and open to new businesses.

💬 Response (13,240 in / 889 out)
Here is what you need to know and do.

## What you have now

With pre-settled status, you can already:

- work in the UK
- use the NHS for free
- study or enrol in education
- access public funds such as benefits and pensions, if you are eligible
- travel in and out of the UK

## Your most important next step: apply for settled status

You have lived in the UK since 2018, so you have likely already reached 5 continuous years of residence. This means you should now apply to upgrade from pre-settled status to settled status. It is free to apply and there is no deadline for this upgrade.

Settled status gives you the permanent right to live in the UK and makes it much easier to prove your rights to employers, landlords and public services. It also opens the door to applying for British citizenship if you want to in future.

You can apply at [Convert pre-settled status to settled status on GOV.UK](https://www.gov.uk/settled-status-eu-citizens-families/convert-pre-settled-status-to-settled-status).

Note: if you do not apply, your pre-settled status will be automatically extended by 5 years just before it expires, and the Home Office may convert it to settled status automatically. However, it is strongly recommended that you apply yourself as soon as you are eligible, rather than waiting.

## Set up your UKVI account and eVisa

Your immigration status is now held digitally as an eVisa. You need a UKVI account to view it and to get a share code, which you use to prove your right to work, rent a home, or access services.

Set up or access your account at [eVisas: access and use your online immigration status](https://www.gov.uk/evisa).

## What to do, in order

1. Check you have a UKVI account and can view your eVisa
2. Apply to upgrade to settled status (it is free)
3. Once you have settled status, consider whether you want to apply for British citizenship

Start by applying to upgrade your status at [the EU Settlement Scheme page on GOV.UK](https://www.gov.uk/settled-status-eu-citizens-families).

💬 Response (14,591 in / 651 out)
Here is a clear picture of what you should be doing now and what you can work towards.

## Your most important next step — upgrade to settled status

You have pre-settled status, which means you have lived in the UK for less than 5 continuous years. Once you reach 5 continuous years of residence (which for you will be around 2023 if you arrived in 2018), you can and should upgrade to full settled status. This is the most important thing to do.

- Apply to upgrade via the EU Settlement Scheme — it is free
- You must apply before your pre-settled status expires (check your status document for the exact date)
- You can apply at gov.uk/settled-status-eu-citizens-families

## Proving your status now

Your immigration status is now digital — there is no physical card for EUSS holders.

- Set up a UKVI account and access your eVisa at gov.uk/get-access-evisa
- Use this to generate a share code to prove your right to work or rent to employers and landlords
- View or share your status at gov.uk/view-prove-immigration-status

## What you are entitled to with pre-settled status

- You can live and work in the UK without restriction
- You can access the NHS — register with a GP at gov.uk/register-with-a-gp if you have not already
- You can claim most benefits and public funds you are eligible for (subject to the usual rules)
- You can travel in and out of the UK, but avoid long absences as these can break your continuous residence

## Your route to settled status and beyond

1. Reach 5 years of continuous UK residence and apply for settled status (EU Settlement Scheme)
2. Once you have settled status, wait 12 months, then apply for British citizenship (naturalisation) if you wish — this costs £1,735 and requires passing the Life in the UK test
3. If approved for citizenship, attend a citizenship ceremony within 3 months — you can then apply for a British passport

## Important — do not let your pre-settled status lapse

If your pre-settled status expires before you apply to upgrade, you could lose your right to live in the UK. Check your expiry date now and apply to upgrade as soon as you are eligible.

For full details on the EU Settlement Scheme, visit gov.uk/settled-status-eu-citizens-families.

💬 Response (14,098 in / 732 out)
Good news — you do not need to panic about your pre-settled status expiring. Here is what the current GOV.UK guidance says.

## Your status will be extended automatically

Just before your pre-settled status expires, the Home Office will automatically extend it by 5 years. You will get an email when this happens. You do not need to do anything to trigger this extension.

After the extension, the Home Office may also automatically convert your status to settled status, if they are satisfied you still meet the requirements.

However, the Home Office can cancel the extension or choose not to convert your status if they believe you no longer qualify.

## What you should do instead: apply now

Because you have lived in the UK since 2018, you have almost certainly already completed 5 continuous years of residence. This means you are very likely eligible for settled status right now. It is strongly recommended that you apply yourself rather than waiting for an automatic conversion, because:

- it gives you certainty and proof of your permanent right to stay
- it protects your access to work, the NHS, benefits, and renting a home
- it is free to apply and there is no deadline for this upgrade

## How to apply

1. Go to the [EU Settlement Scheme page on GOV.UK](https://www.gov.uk/settled-status-eu-citizens-families/convert-pre-settled-status-to-settled-status)
2. Sign in to your UKVI account
3. Submit your application to upgrade to settled status

Apply as soon as possible at [the EU Settlement Scheme: convert pre-settled to settled status](https://www.gov.uk/settled-status-eu-citizens-families/convert-pre-settled-status-to-settled-status).

💬 Response (19,050 in / 583 out)
The good news is that your pre-settled status will not simply expire and leave you without protection. Here is what you need to know.

## Your status will be extended automatically

The Home Office will automatically extend your pre-settled status by 5 years before it expires. You will receive an email when this happens. You do not need to do anything to trigger this extension.

## What you should do now — upgrade to settled status

Even with the automatic extension, you should apply to upgrade to full settled status as soon as you are eligible. You are eligible once you have 5 continuous years of UK residence.

As you arrived in 2018, you should already be eligible. Here is what to do:

1. Check your continuous residence — make sure you have not had any single absence of more than 6 months, or total absences of more than 12 months in any 5-year period
2. Gather evidence of your UK residence (payslips, bank statements, council tax bills, GP letters, etc.)
3. Apply for settled status for free at gov.uk/settled-status-eu-citizens-families

## Make sure your eVisa is set up

While you sort out your status, make sure you can access and prove it digitally.

- Set up or check your UKVI account at gov.uk/get-access-evisa
- Use it to generate a share code to prove your right to work or rent at gov.uk/view-prove-immigration-status

## If you have a problem with your status

If your status is not showing correctly or you have a question about your application, you can contact the Home Office EUSS Resolution Centre at gov.uk/contact-ukvi-inside-outside-uk.

The single most important action is to apply for settled status now — visit gov.uk/settled-status-eu-citizens-families to start your free application.
